import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    A simple model that performs a cumulative sum (prefix sum) operation along a specified dimension.

    Parameters:
        dim (int): The dimension along which to perform the scan operation.
    """

    def __init__(self, dim):
        """
        Initialize the Scan model.

        Args:
            dim (int): The dimension along which to perform the cumulative sum.
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.dim = dim

    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Forward pass for the Scan model, computing the cumulative sum along the specified dimension.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, *input_shape), where `*input_shape` 
                              can vary depending on the use case.

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Tensor of the same shape as `x` after applying cumulative sum along `dim`.
        """
        return torch.cumsum(x, dim=self.dim)

# Define input dimensions and parameters
batch_size = 32768
input_shape = (32768,)
dim = 1

def get_inputs():
    """
    Generates random inputs for testing the Scan model.

    Returns:
        list: A list containing a single randomly generated tensor with shape 
              (batch_size, *input_shape).
    """
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, *input_shape)]

def get_init_inputs():
    """
    Returns the initialization parameters for the Scan model.

    Returns:
        list: A list containing the `dim` parameter for model initialization.
    """
    return [dim]

The user wants me to write a custom CUDA kernel to replace `torch.cumsum` for inputs of shape `(32768, 32768)` along dimension 1. This is essentially a 2D prefix sum where each row needs to be cumulatively summed independently. First, let's get the GPU specs to understand what we're working with. We need to optimize for memory bandwidth and parallelism. Key considerations: 1. Input shape: (32768, 32768) = about 1 billion elements (4 GB for fp32) 2. Operation: cumsum along dim=1 (row-wise) 3. This is a memory bandwidth bound problem 4. Standard parallel scan algorithms (Blelloch, Kogge-Stone) can be used within blocks 5. For such large dimensions, we need to process each row in parallel Let me think about the best approach: - Each row has 32768 elements - We can assign one block per row (32768 blocks) - Each block does a parallel scan on 32768 elements - 32768 elements per row / 1024 threads = 32 elements per thread - This is a classic problem for CUDA A good approach is: 1. Grid: (32768, 1, 1) - one block per row 2. Block: 1024 threads (or 512) 3. Each thread loads multiple elements into shared memory 4. Perform parallel scan in shared memory using the Hillis-Steele or Blelloch algorithm 5. Write results back Wait, 32768 elements per row with 1024 threads means 32 elements per thread. Actually, let me calculate shared memory: 32768 * 4 bytes = 128 KB per block. That's too much for shared memory (usually 48-100KB per SM). Alternative approach: - Process each row in tiles/chunks - Each block processes a tile of, say, 1024 or 2048 elements - Use sequential scan within a thread for its elements, then broadcast the offset to subsequent tiles - Or use a two-level scan: first scan tiles, then add offsets Actually, since we have 32768 rows and each row is 32768 elements, let me think about a multi-pass approach or a tiled approach. Wait, maybe we can have each thread process a contiguous chunk sequentially. If we have 1024 threads per block and process each row with one block: - Each thread handles 32768/1024 = 32 elements sequentially - Thread i computes the sum and writes it out - Then we need a parallel scan across the 1024 thread partial sums - Then each thread adds the prefix offset to its elements This uses much less shared memory: 1024 * 4 = 4 KB for the block-level scan. Algorithm per row (one block per row): 1. Each thread loads `elements_per_thread` (32) elements from its assigned chunk into registers 2. Each thread computes the prefix sum of its 32 elements sequentially, storing results back 3. Each thread computes the total sum of its 32 elements 4. Threads participate in a block-level parallel scan of the 1024 thread sums 5. Each thread reads the prefix sum from preceding threads 6. Each thread adds this prefix to all its 32 elements 7. Write back to global memory This is efficient because: - Each thread does sequential work on 32 elements (good memory coalescing, computationally dense) - Block scan is small and fast (1024 elements) - Coalesced memory access Let me think about memory access patterns: - Input is (32768, 32768), row-major - Reading along dim=1 means contiguous memory access - Threads within a warp access contiguous elements if thread i reads elements [i*32 : (i+1)*32] - Wait, that's not coalesced if each thread reads 32 contiguous elements. Actually it IS coalesced - consecutive threads access consecutive memory. Thread 0 reads 0-31, thread 1 reads 32-63, etc. Actually, for coalesced access: - Thread 0 should read element 0, thread 1 should read element 1, etc. - OR thread 0 reads a contiguous chunk but warps need to be aligned Since each thread handles 32 contiguous elements and threads are mapped to contiguous chunks: - Thread 0: elements 0-31 - Thread 1: elements 32-63 - ... This gives coalesced access because thread 0 starts at 0, thread 1 at 32, etc. Within a warp (32 threads), the addresses are 0, 32, 64, ..., 992, 1, 33, 65... No wait, if each thread reads in a loop: Thread 0: reads 0, then 1, then 2... Thread 1: reads 32, then 33, then 34... Actually, better approach for coalescing: Have thread i in the block read element i, i+blockDim.x, i+2*blockDim.x, etc. - Thread 0 reads elements 0, 1024, 2048... - Thread 1 reads elements 1, 1025, 2049... This way, all threads in the warp read contiguous elements at each load. So algorithm: 1. Thread `tid` reads elements `tid + k*blockDim.x` for k=0..31 2. Thread sequentially prefixes its 32 elements 3. Thread writes the sum of all its elements to shared memory 4. Parallel scan on the 1024 sums in shared memory 5. Thread reads its offset from shared memory 6. Thread adds offset to each of its 32 elements and writes back This gives perfect coalescing and uses very little shared memory. Let's design the block-level scan. 1024 elements in shared memory. Option 1: Kogge-Stone scan - needs 1024 * 4 = 4KB shared memory, log2(1024)=10 steps Option 2: Brent-Kung scan - needs about 2*1024 elements shared memory, fewer steps For 1024 elements, Kogge-Stone with 1024 threads is straightforward: ```cpp __shared__ float sdata[1024]; sdata[tid] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); for (int offset = 1; offset < blockDim.x; offset *= 2) { float val = 0; if (tid >= offset) val = sdata[tid - offset]; __syncthreads(); if (tid >= offset) sdata[tid] += val; __syncthreads(); } ``` Wait, we need prefix sum, not inclusive scan of the thread sums. After the above, `sdata[tid]` contains the inclusive scan. Then the offset for thread `tid` is `sdata[tid] - local_sum` for exclusive, or the thread before it. Actually: - After inclusive scan in sdata: sdata[tid] = sum of thread sums 0..tid - Thread tid's exclusive prefix = sdata[tid] - local_sum (sum of all previous threads) Yes. Let me refine the algorithm: ```cpp __global__ void row_cumsum_kernel(const float* input, float* output, int rows, int cols) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int blockSize = blockDim.x; if (row >= rows) return; const float* row_in = input + row * cols; float* row_out = output + row * cols; // Each thread handles elements at: tid, tid+blockSize, tid+2*blockSize, ... int num_iters = cols / blockSize; // 32768 / 1024 = 32 float local_vals[32]; // stored in registers float local_sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < num_iters; i++) { int idx = tid + i * blockSize; float val = row_in[idx]; local_sum += val; local_vals[i] = local_sum; } // Block-level exclusive scan on local_sums __shared__ float sdata[1024]; sdata[tid] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); // Kogge-Stone inclusive scan #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 1024; offset *= 2) { float val = 0.0f; if (tid >= offset) { val = sdata[tid - offset]; } __syncthreads(); if (tid >= offset) { sdata[tid] += val; } __syncthreads(); } float exclusive_prefix = (tid > 0) ? sdata[tid - 1] : 0.0f; // For the entire block: what about block-level exclusive prefix? // Actually for a single block per row, exclusive_prefix is the offset from previous threads #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < num_iters; i++) { int idx = tid + i * blockSize; row_out[idx] = local_vals[i] + exclusive_prefix; } } ``` Wait, there's a subtle issue. The local_vals[i] stores the running sum within the thread. After adding exclusive_prefix (sum of all previous threads' total sums), we get the correct prefix sum. This is strided by 2, which might not be fully coalesced but still okay for modern GPUs. Better yet, let threads read in a coalesced manner. For 1024 threads and 32 elements per thread = 32768 elements per row: A warp of 32 threads reads elements 0, 32, 64, 96... for their first element. That's a stride of 32 * 4 bytes = 128 bytes, which is bad for coalescing. To fix coalescing, we can use shared memory to transpose the reads. But that adds complexity. So when a warp executes, threads 0-31 should read addresses 0-31, then 32-63, etc. If each thread reads 32 elements, and we want warp-level coalescing: - Warp 0 (threads 0-31): each thread reads one element. The scan of tile k needs the total sum of tiles 0..k-1. So a warp-level or block-level approach: - Process the row in tiles of, say, 1024 elements - Each tile is loaded into shared memory and scanned in parallel - Meanwhile, compute tile sums and do a separate scan on tile sums to get offsets - Then add offsets to each tile This is a common two-level scan algorithm. Load tile 1, scan it, add offset, write back. etc. This processes tiles sequentially, which is simple but threads do a lot of synchronization. Option B: Two-level parallel scan. - First pass: each thread block processes some part of the row and computes local prefix sums and local total sum. - Second pass: scan the local total sums to get offsets. - Third pass: add offsets to local prefix sums. This is more complex but fully parallel. For large rows (32768 elements), another approach is to have warps do sequential scan on chunks, then do a block-level scan on warp sums. Actually, let's look at what happens with warp-level processing. Modern GPUs have warp shuffle instructions! So for memory coalescing, we absolutely want consecutive threads to access consecutive memory. But is a parallel scan on a tile in shared memory efficient? For tile size = 1024 = block size: 1. Load 1024 elements into smem 2. Up-sweep phase of Blelloch scan (inclusive scan): - stride = 1: threads 1,3,5,... do nothing? No, Kogge-stone: - Step 1 (offset=1): thread i reads smem[i-1], smem[i] += smem[i-1] - Step 2 (offset=2): thread i reads smem[i-2], smem[i] += smem[i-2] - ... 3. This requires log2(1024) = 10 rounds 4. Each round: __syncthreads, then loads/updates For 32 tiles per row, that's 32 * 10 = 320 rounds of barrier synchronization per block. That might be okay but could be bottlenecked. Alternative within a tile: we can use warp shuffles for the scan within each warp, then warp-level aggregates scanned across warps. - Tile size = 1024 = 32 warps * 32 threads - Each warp does a parallel scan on 32 elements using shfl - Warp sums are written to shared memory - Block-level scan on 32 warp sums - Each warp adds its offset using shfl_broadcast This reduces the number of __syncthreads() needed. Let's count: - 32 warps, each does shfl scan on 32 elements: 5 shfl steps per warp, no syncthreads - Write warp sums to smem: 1 syncthreads - Scan 32 warp sums: can do in one warp with shfl, or in smem. For 32 elements, one warp (warp 0) can scan them with shfl in 5 steps. - Broadcast offsets: 1 syncthreads (to get back to all warps) - Each warp adds offset: no syncthreads Total: 3 syncthreads per tile. For 32 tiles: 96 syncthreads per block. Better than 320. But this is still processing tiles sequentially. In Ampere/Hopper, a warp executing 32 threads with addresses `base + lane * stride` can coalesce into multiple transactions depending on strid. With stride=128 bytes and 32 threads, this is exactly 32 cache lines (one per thread), so 32 memory transactions. That's terrible compared to 1 transaction for contiguous access. To fix this, we process memory in tiles that are transposed through shared memory, or we change our algorithm. For tile size = block size = 1024: We can compute the scan with a Brent-Kung algorithm which uses fewer steps and slightly more shared memory, but same number of syncthreads. Let's flesh this out. Algorithm per row (one block = 1024 threads, 32 warps): 1. The row has `cols = 32768` elements = 1024 chunks of 32 elements 2. Thread block processes all 1024 chunks in 32 sequential steps 3. At step `s` (0 <= s < 32): - Warp `w` (0 <= w < 32) loads elements at: `row_start + s * 1024 + w * 32 + lane` - Lane 0..31 in warp w loads `s * 1024 + w * 32 + lane` - Warp performs inclusive scan on these 32 values using shfl - Add block offset (running sum from previous steps) - Store results back to same positions - Update block offset with the sum of this chunk Wait, but warp 0 in step 0 scans its 32 elements. Then in step 1, warp 0 reads elements 1024..1055 and adds total from all warps in step 0. Let's verify: Step 0: - Warp 0 scans elements 0..31 -> [a0, a0+a1, ..., sum(a0..a31)] - Warp 1 scans elements 32..63 -> [a32, a32+a33, ..., sum(a32..a63)] - ... - Warp 31 scans elements 992..1023 -> [a992, ..., sum(a992..a1023)] - Block offset after step 0 = sum(a0..a1023) Step 1: - Warp 0 scans elements 1024..1055 -> [b1024, b1024+b1025, ..., sum(b1024..b1055)] - Warp 0 adds block_offset to each: [sum(a0..a1023)+b1024, ...] - This is correct! - Block offset after step 1 += sum(b1024..b1055) So this works perfectly and: 1. So we need: 1. Prefix from all previous steps (block_offset) 2. PLUS prefix from previous warps within the current step Let me re-examine. In step 0, there's no previous step, so block_offset = 0. If we're processing a step of 1024 elements with 32 warps of 32 threads each, warps within the same step process DIFFERENT chunks of the step. So warp 1's results need warp 0's total sum. Let me recalculate. A step of 1024 elements: - Warp 0: indices [0..31] - Warp 1: indices [32..63] - ... - Warp 31: indices [992..1023] If we just do warp-level scan within each chunk, warp 1's output would be incorrect because it doesn't include warp 0's sum. So at each step, we need: 1. Each warp scans its 32-element chunk 2. Each warp gets its chunk's total sum 3. We do a block-level scan on the 32 chunk sums to get per-warp offsets 4. Each warp adds its offset to its elements 5. Then the total sum of this step is computed and added to block_offset for next step Now, step 1: - Warp 0: indices [1024..1055], needs block_offset (sum of step 0) - Warp 1: indices [1056..1087], needs block_offset + warp 0's sum in step 1 - etc. So we DO need block-level scan on the 32 warp totals at each step. This requires some synchronization. Let me detail step s: ```cpp float val = input[base + warp_id * 32 + lane]; // perfectly coalesced float scan_val = warp_inclusive_scan(val); // shfl scan // Get warp total (broadcast from lane 31) float warp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, scan_val, 31); // Store warp_sum to shared memory for block-level scan __shared__ float warp_sums[32]; if (lane == 0) warp_sums[warp_id] = warp_sum; __syncthreads(); // Block-level exclusive scan on 32 warp sums // Can use warp 0 to do this since 32 elements = 1 warp float warp_offset = 0; if (warp_id == 0) { // Inclusive or exclusive scan on warp_sums, then store back // Actually just do it in one warp and broadcast } __syncthreads(); float offset = block_offset + warp_offset; scan_val += offset; // Write back output[base + warp_id * 32 + lane] = scan_val; // Update block_offset __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { block_offset += warp_sums[31]; // last element after scan } __syncthreads(); ``` Wait, `warp_sums[31]` after the block-level scan is the total sum of the step. So we need to store and read it. For the block-level scan on 32 elements with warp 0: Since there are exactly 32 warp sums, warp 0 can do a warp scan on them. ```cpp // Lane 0 of each warp stored the sum __shared__ float warp_sums[32]; if (warp_id == 0) { float x = (lane < 32) ? warp_sums[lane] : 0; float scanned = warp_inclusive_scan(x); warp_sums[lane] = scanned; // now inclusive scan } __syncthreads(); float warp_offset = (warp_id > 0) ? warp_sums[warp_id - 1] : 0; ``` This gives the exclusive scan of warp totals. Then `block_offset` is the total from all previous steps. ```cpp __shared__ float block_offset_smem; if (threadIdx.x == 0) block_offset_smem = 0; __syncthreads(); // loop over steps for (int s = 0; s < num_steps; s++) { int base = s * 1024; // 1024 = blockDim.x // load from global float val = input[row * cols + base + warp_id * 32 + lane]; // warp inclusive scan float scanned = warp_inclusive_scan(val); // get warp sum float warp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, scanned, 31); // store to smem if (lane == 0) warp_sums[warp_id] = warp_sum; __syncthreads(); // block-level exclusive scan with warp 0 if (warp_id == 0) { float x = warp_sums[lane]; float scanned2 = warp_inclusive_scan(x); warp_sums[lane] = scanned2; } __syncthreads(); float step_offset = (warp_id > 0) ? warp_sums[warp_id - 1] : 0.0f; float local_offset = block_offset_smem + step_offset; scanned += local_offset; // store to global output[row * cols + base + warp_id * 32 + lane] = scanned; // update block_offset __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { block_offset_smem += warp_sums[31]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` Let's trace through: Step 0, block_offset_smem = 0. Warp 0 scans [a0..a31], scanned[31] = sum(a0..a31). warp_sum0 = sum(a0..a31). ... Warp 31 scans [a992..a1023], scanned[31] = sum(a992..a1023). After warp 0 scan of warp_sums: warp_sums[0] = sum0 warp_sums[1] = sum0+sum1 ... warp_sums[31] = sum0+...+sum31 = total step 0 sum warp_offset for warp 0 = 0 (warp_id=0) warp_offset for warp 1 = warp_sums[0] = sum0 warp_offset for warp 2 = warp_sums[1] = sum0+sum1 Element output: warp 0, lane 0: a0 + 0 + 0 = a0 ✓ warp 0, lane 31: sum(a0..a31) + 0 + 0 = sum(a0..a31) ✓ warp 1, lane 0: a32 + 0 + sum0 = sum0 + a32 = sum(a0..a31)+a32 = sum(a0..a32) ✓ warp 1, lane 31: sum(a32..a63) + sum0 = sum(a0..a63) ✓ Then block_offset_smem = warp_sums[31] = total step 0 sum. Step 1: offset = block_offset_smem + step_offset warp 0, lane 0: b1024 + total0 + 0 = total0 + b1024 ✓ warp 1, lane 0: b1056 + total0 + sum(b1024..b1055) = total0 + b1024 + ... + b1056 ✓ This is correct! Now, `warp_inclusive_scan`: ```cpp __device__ __forceinline__ float warp_inclusive_scan(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); if (threadIdx.x % 32 >= offset) { val += y; } } return val; } ``` Actually, `lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32` which is correct. But we can use `(lane >= offset)` condition. To avoid threadIdx.x modulo, we can pass lane explicitly. ```cpp __device__ __forceinline__ float warp_inclusive_scan(float val, int lane) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); if (lane >= offset) val += y; } return val; } ``` This uses 5 shuffles. For 32768 cols, we have 32768 / 1024 = 32 steps. 32 steps * 7 syncthreads per step = 224 syncthreads per block. Is that too many? Each syncthreads is cheap, but it might add up. Actually, we can optimize by computing block_offset update after syncthreads where warp_sums already has inclusive scan. lane 0 can read warp_sums[31] after the second syncthreads. Wait, actually we can reduce syncthreads: ```cpp // At each step: 1. load & warp scan 2. lane 0 stores warp_sum to smem 3. __syncthreads() // need so warp 0 sees all warp sums 4. warp 0 scans warp_sums 5. __syncthreads() // need so all warps see scanned sums 6. compute offset and write global 7. if (threadIdx.x == 0) block_offset_smem += warp_sums[31]; 8. __syncthreads() // need so next iteration starts with updated block_offset ``` That's 3 syncthreads per step. 96 total. Should be fine. Can we avoid the third syncthreads by having the last warp update it before writing back? Actually, after step 5, all warps can read warp_sums. Lane 0 of warp 31 knows warp_sums[31] (the total). But we need all threads to wait for the update before next step. So yes, syncthreads is needed. Actually, we can reorder: After step 5, we know warp_sums[warp_id-1]. Instead of a separate syncthreads for block_offset, we can have all threads participate in computing the new block_offset, or we can note that block_offset for step s+1 is the same for all threads. Hmmm. What if we store ALL step totals in an array and do a second pass? That would be a two-pass algorithm avoiding the sequential dependency. Two-pass algorithm: Pass 1: Compute per-warp inclusive scans, write back. Also compute per-step totals in parallel and store them. Pass 2: Scan the step totals to get offsets, then add offsets to all elements. For a row: - We have 32 steps, each of 1024 elements processed by the block. - In pass 1, for each step we do the warp scan and save, and save the step total (the last scanned value of the last warp plus its offset... wait, we'd need the offset to know the total). Actually, without sequential block_offset, pass 1 can just compute the raw per-warp scans, and compute the total sum of each step (sum of all warp sums in that step). So step_totals[step] = sum of all elements in that step. Then scan step_totals to get step_offsets. Then pass 2: add step_offsets[step] + warp_offset_within_step to each element. This eliminates the sequential dependency across steps! Both passes can be independent. Pass 1 details: ```cpp // For each step in row for (int s = 0; s < 32; s++) { int idx = row * cols + s * 1024 + warp_id * 32 + lane; float val = input[idx]; float scanned = warp_inclusive_scan(val, lane); // Write scanned to output temporarily (or to temp buffer) output[idx] = scanned; float warp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, scanned, 31); if (lane == 0) warp_sums[warp_id] = warp_sum; __syncthreads(); // Step total = sum of all warp_sums // We can use warp 0 to sum 32 elements... or just reduce // Actually, step_totals[s] = warp_sums[0] + ... + warp_sums[31] if (warp_id == 0) { // reduction using warp shuffle float x = warp_sums[lane]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { x += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, x, offset); } if (lane == 0) step_totals[s] = x; } __syncthreads(); } ``` Then scan step_totals[0..31]: This is a 32-element scan, which can be done by one warp very fast. ```cpp // Thread 0 does the scan __shared__ float step_offsets[32]; if (threadIdx.x < 32) { float val = (threadIdx.x < 32) ? step_totals[threadIdx.x] : 0; float scanned = warp_inclusive_scan(val, threadIdx.x); step_offsets[threadIdx.x] = scanned; } __syncthreads(); ``` Wait, we need exclusive scan for step offsets: step s offset = sum of step_totals[0..s-1]. So step_offsets[0] = 0, step_offsets[1] = step_totals[0], step_offsets[2] = step_totals[0]+step_totals[1], etc. That's just an exclusive scan. Or inclusive scan shifted. ```cpp float exclusive = (lane > 0) ? scanned[lane-1] : 0; ``` Then Pass 2: ```cpp for (int s = 0; s < 32; s++) { int idx = row * cols + s * 1024 + warp_id * 32 + lane; float val = output[idx]; // currently just intra-warp scan float warp_sum = ... // we need warp_offsets again // Actually, recompute warp_offset within step s // We can recompute it the same way as before using warp_sums // But we already have the raw warp sums? No, output[idx] is the intra-warp scan. float raw = (lane > 0) ? __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, lane) : 0; // No that's not easy. // Alternative: we stored the raw per-warp scan in output. // We need each warp's sum again. We can recompute from output: float warp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val + (lane < 31 ? __shfl_up(val, lane+1) ... no). // Actually, the last lane has the warp sum. // We can just recompute warp_sums by having lane 31 broadcast. float warp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 31); if (lane == 0) warp_sums[warp_id] = warp_sum; __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float x = warp_sums[lane]; float scanned = warp_inclusive_scan(x, lane); warp_sums[lane] = scanned; } __syncthreads(); float warp_offset = (warp_id > 0) ? warp_sums[warp_id - 1] : 0; float step_offset = (s > 0) ? step_offsets[s - 1] : 0; // wait, step_offsets needs to be from step_totals scan } ``` This two-pass approach has more operations and memory traffic, so it's probably worse than the single-pass sequential step approach. Let me reconsider the single-pass step approach. 3 syncthreads per step and 32 steps = 96 syncthreads. At 5-10 cycles each, that's ~500-1000 cycles. Memory reads: 32 * 1024 reads = 32768 reads. At 4 bytes each, 128KB of reads per row. For 32768 rows, total reads = 4GB. At 1TB/s, that's 4ms just for memory, so 96 syncthreads is negligible. So the single-pass approach should be fine and is simpler. However, there's a concern: 32768 blocks might be too many? No, that's great for occupancy. A100/H100 has 108/132 SMs, each can run multiple blocks. Wait, each block processes 32768 floats = reads 4 bytes * 32768 = 128KB per block. With 1024 threads, that's 128 bytes per thread. Actually, the biggest concern is whether this kernel is faster than torch.cumsum. PyTorch's cumsum might use similar techniques or even a highly tuned cub::DeviceScan. Let me think... `torch.cumsum` along dim=1 for a large 2D tensor often delegates to a specialized CUDA kernel. For contiguous dimensions, it might be very fast. But since PyTorch needs to handle arbitrary dimensions and strides, there might be overhead. One trick: for a tensor of shape (32768, 32768) doing cumsum on dim=1, each row is completely independent. That's ideal for parallelism. Actually, I just realized we might get better performance with a slightly different approach: using vectorized loads (float2 or float4) to reduce instruction count and improve bandwidth. Let's use float4 loads when possible. 32768 is divisible by 4, so we can process 4 elements per thread per load. If we process 4 elements at a time per thread, we need to handle the sequential prefix within the vector. For warp-level approach with float4: - Each thread handles 4 consecutive elements: lane processes [base + lane*4, base + lane*4 + 3] - Warp handles 128 elements (32 threads * 4) - Load float4: `float4 v = ((float4*)ptr)[lane];` - Sequential prefix within the 4 elements: v.y += v.x; v.z += v.y; v.w += v.z; - Then warp scan on the vector totals (v.w) - Then apply offsets to all elements in the vector. Memory access with float4: each warp reads 128 consecutive floats = 512 bytes. That's perfectly coalesced (16 consecutive 32-byte transactions or similar, depending on architecture). With float4, a row of 32768 elements = 256 chunks of 128 elements. 32 warps, each handles 128 elements. So we need 256/32 = 8 steps. Only 8 iterations! That's much better than 32 steps, reducing loop overhead and syncthreads. Let's design with float4: - 1024 threads = 32 warps * 32 threads - Each step: each warp processes 128 elements via float4 - Total steps per row: 32768 / (32 warps * 128 elements) = 32768 / 4096 = 8 steps Wait, 1024 threads * 4 floats/thread = 4096 floats per step. 32768 / 4096 = 8 steps. Yes. 8 steps * 3 syncthreads = 24 syncthreads per block. Very manageable. This should be very fast. Let me detail the float4 kernel: ```cpp struct float4 { float x, y, z, w; }; ``` Actually, CUDA has built-in `float4`. But I need to be careful: `float4` loads require 16-byte alignment. Since each row is contiguous and starts at a float boundary, and we split by `warp_id * 128 + lane * 4`, each warp processes 128 aligned floats starting at `base + warp_id * 128`. Since 128 * 4 = 512 bytes, and row start is 4-byte aligned, warp_id * 128 is 512-byte aligned, which is divisible by 16. So float4 access is aligned. Good. Wait, actually `base + warp_id * 128 + lane * 4`. Warp 0: offsets 0, 4, 8, ... 124 from base. These are all aligned. Warp 1: offsets 128, 132, ... 252. Also aligned. Yes. Algorithm with float4 per step: ```cpp __device__ __forceinline__ float warp_inclusive_scan(float val, int lane) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); if (lane >= offset) val += y; } return val; } __global__ void row_cumsum_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int rows, int cols) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= rows) return; int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; const float4* in4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(input + row * cols); float4* out4 = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(output + row * cols); __shared__ float warp_sums[32]; __shared__ float block_offset; if (threadIdx.x == 0) block_offset = 0.0f; __syncthreads(); int num_steps = cols / (blockDim.x * 4); // 32768 / 4096 = 8 #pragma unroll for (int step = 0; step < num_steps; step++) { int idx = step * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; // global float4 index float4 v = in4[idx]; // Sequential prefix within the 4 elements v.y += v.x; v.z += v.y; v.w += v.z; float val = v.w; // total of this thread's 4 elements // Warp-level inclusive scan on totals float scanned = warp_inclusive_scan(val, lane); // Total for this warp float warp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, scanned, 31); if (lane == 0) warp_sums[warp_id] = warp_sum; __syncthreads(); // Block-level inclusive scan on 32 warp sums (warp 0 does it) if (warp_id == 0) { float x = warp_sums[lane]; float s = warp_inclusive_scan(x, lane); warp_sums[lane] = s; } __syncthreads(); // Offset for this warp within this step and block float step_offset = (warp_id > 0) ? warp_sums[warp_id - 1] : 0.0f; float offset = block_offset + step_offset; // scanned contains inclusive scan of totals up to this thread. // The total prefix up to but not including this thread's first element is: float prefix_before = offset + scanned - val; v.x += prefix_before; v.y += prefix_before; v.z += prefix_before; v.w += prefix_before; out4[idx] = v; // Update block_offset for next step if (threadIdx.x == 0) { block_offset = warp_sums[31] + block_offset; } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Let me trace this carefully. Step 0, block_offset = 0. Thread (warp 0, lane 0): v = [a0, a1, a2, a3]. v.w = a0+a1+a2+a3. val = sum(a0..a3) scanned = sum(a0..a3) (since lane 0) warp_sum for warp 0 = __shfl(scanned, 31). Let me write the code. ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cuda_fp16.h> __device__ __forceinline__ float warp_inclusive_scan(float val, int lane) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); if (lane >= offset) { val += y; } } return val; } __global__ void row_cumsum_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int rows, int cols) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= rows) return; int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; const float4* in4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(input + row * cols); float4* out4 = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(output + row * cols); __shared__ float warp_sums[32]; __shared__ float block_offset; if (threadIdx.x == 0) block_offset = 0.0f; __syncthreads(); int elems_per_step = blockDim.x * 4; int num_steps = cols / elems_per_step; #pragma unroll for (int step = 0; step < num_steps; step++) { int idx = step * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; float4 v = in4[idx]; v.y += v.x; v.z += v.y; v.w += v.z; float val = v.w; float scanned = warp_inclusive_scan(val, lane); float warp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, scanned, 31); if (lane == 0) { warp_sums[warp_id] = warp_sum; } __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float x = warp_sums[lane]; float s = warp_inclusive_scan(x, lane); warp_sums[lane] = s; } __syncthreads(); float step_offset = (warp_id > 0) ? The task is to implement a fast cumulative sum (prefix sum) operation along dimension 1 for a tensor of shape (32768, 32768). This is a 2D tensor, and we need to compute the prefix sum along the second dimension (dim=1). So each row of 32768 elements needs to be prefix-summed independently. Let's think about the memory access pattern. The input tensor is (32768, 32768) = 1,073,741,824 elements ≈ 4GB in fp32. That's quite large, but fits in H100's 80GB memory. Wait, (32768, 32768) is exactly 2^30 elements. In fp32, that's 4GB. The output is also 4GB. So we're doing an in-place or out-of-place prefix sum across 4GB of data. The naive PyTorch approach `torch.cumsum(x, dim=1)` will likely use some optimized implementation, but might not be fully optimized for contiguous memory access along the inner dimension. Since each row is independent and contiguous in memory (since dim=1 is the inner dimension for a (32768, 32768) tensor in row-major layout), we can write a very efficient kernel. The key insight is that for a (32768, 32768) tensor with row-major layout, elements along dim=1 are contiguous. So each row is a contiguous block of 32768 floats. We need to do 32768 independent prefix sums, each on a contiguous array of 32768 floats. Using shared memory for efficient parallelization: 1. Each block handles one row (or multiple rows) 2. Within a block, we use a parallel scan algorithm in shared memory 3. For large arrays like 32768 elements, we might need multiple phases But wait, 32768 elements = 32768 * 4 bytes = 128KB per row. Let me think about the algorithm more carefully: Single block scan algorithm for float32: 1. Each thread loads multiple elements from global memory to shared memory 2. Write back to global memory For 32768 elements and 256 threads per block: - Each thread processes 128 elements - Shared memory needed: 32768 * 4 = 128KB < 227KB limit. Good. - But we need even more for the scan algorithm itself. Actually, if we use the classic Blelloch scan in shared memory, we'd need 2 * 32768 * 4 bytes = 256KB. That's too much. We can use a different approach: 1. Thread-level sequential scan: each thread scans its own chunk of 128 elements, storing the local sum 2. Block-level scan of the local sums 3. Add the prefix sums back This is the hierarchical scan approach and uses much less shared memory. Algorithm details: - Block size: 256 threads - Elements per thread: 128 - Elements per block: 256 * 128 = 32768 (one row) - Shared memory: 256 * 4 bytes for block-level sums + whatever for temporary storage Actually, we can also process multiple rows per block to amortize the block-level scan overhead. Alternative: Since the elements per row (32768) is large, we can also process each row with multiple blocks. This is useful if we have many rows and want to launch even more parallelism. But 32768 rows * 32768 elements = 1B elements, and with each block handling 32768 elements via 256 threads, we'd have 32768 blocks. That's already a lot. Actually, we could also do a multipass algorithm: - Phase 1: Each block processes a tile of 1024 elements per row, writes the tile sum - Phase 2: Scan the tile sums - Phase 3: Add the scanned tile sums back to each tile This would allow us to use fewer shared resources per block and potentially get better occupancy. Let me design a kernel: For an input of shape (M, N) where M=32768, N=32768, and we scan along N. Option A: 1 block per row - Grid: (M, 1) blocks - Each block has T threads (e.g., 256 or 512) - Each thread processes N/T elements sequentially - Shared memory for T partial sums - Do a scan on the T partial sums - Add prefix back This uses T * sizeof(float) ≈ 1-2KB of shared memory, great occupancy! Option B: Multiple blocks per row - Allows more parallelism if N is very large - But requires a 2-phase or 3-phase algorithm For N=32768, with T=256 threads and each thread doing 128 sequential operations, that seems like a good balance between parallelism and sequential work. Actually, let's think about memory bandwidth. Each element needs to be read and written once (for simple scan: read input, write output). In practice, scan might need multiple passes. Actually, we can use warp-level primitives for even faster scan within warps, then combine warps. Let me design a more optimized kernel using warp-shuffle: 1. Add back This is a very common and efficient approach. Detailed kernel design for M=32768, N=32768: ``` __global__ void cumsum_2d_kernel(float* out, const float* in, int M, int N) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int warps_per_block = blockDim.x / 32; if (row >= M) return; const float* row_in = in + row * N; float* row_out = out + row * N; float warp_sum = 0.0f; // Each thread process N/blockDim.x elements int elems_per_thread = (N + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; int start = tid * elems_per_thread; int end = min(start + elems_per_thread, N); // Compute prefix sums within each thread's chunk and warp-level prefix float thread_sum = 0.0f; for (int i = start; i < end; i += 32) { // Each warp collaboratively processes 32 elements // Actually, let's simplify: each thread does its own sequential chunk } } ``` Hmm, let me reconsider. Let me write the CUDA code: ```cuda __global__ void scan_rows_kernel(float* out, const float* in, int M, int N) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int block_size = blockDim.x; if (row >= M) return; const float* input = in + row * N; float* output = out + row * N; float local_vals[64]; // N/block_size = 32768/512 = 64 float prefix = 0.0f; // Load and compute local prefix sums with coalesced access for (int j = 0; j < 64; j++) { int idx = j * block_size + tid; float val = input[idx]; prefix += val; local_vals[j] = prefix; } float thread_sum = prefix; // Block-level exclusive scan of thread_sum using warp shuffle + shared memory // ... // Add block prefix and write back } ``` For the block-level scan, with 512 threads: - Use warp shuffle within warps - Store warp sums in shared memory - Scan warp sums - Add back Let me write the complete block-level scan: ```cuda // Step 1: Warp-level inclusive scan float warp_sum = thread_sum; for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, warp_sum, offset); if (lane >= offset) warp_sum += y; } // Step 2: Store warp sums in shared memory __shared__ float warp_sums[16]; // 512/32 = 16 warps if (lane == 31) warp_sums[warp_id] = warp_sum; __syncthreads(); // Step 3: Warp 0 scans the warp sums if (warp_id == 0) { float val = (lane < 16) ? Let me detail this approach: ### Phase 1: Tile-level inclusive scan - Grid: 32768 rows × 32 tiles = 1,048,576 blocks - Block: 256 threads - Each block processes a tile of 1024 elements - Use shared memory of size 1024 floats = 4KB - Load 1024 elements into shared memory with coalesced access - Do Blelloch or Kogge-Stone scan in shared memory - Write the 1024 scanned elements back - Write the tile total sum (last element of scan) to an auxiliary array Wait, for the Kogge-Stone scan in shared memory with 1024 elements and 256 threads: - Each thread loads 4 elements into shared memory - Then do parallel scan requiring log2(1024) = 10 steps - Each step involves reading and writing shared memory For the Blelloch scan (up-sweep + down-sweep): - Up-sweep: 10 steps of reduction - Down-sweep: 10 steps of distribution - More efficient in terms of total operations Actually, with 256 threads and 1024 elements, each thread can sequentially scan its 4 elements, then we do a block-level scan, then add back. This uses even less shared memory synchronization. Tile scan algorithm (1024 elements, 256 threads): 1. Thread i loads elements i, i+256, i+512, i+768 into registers or shared memory 2. Thread i computes prefix sums: a, a+b, a+b+c, a+b+c+d. Stores the 4 prefix values. Thread_sum = a+b+c+d 3. Store thread_sum in shared memory[256] 4. This is similar to the 1-block-per-row approach, but now each block handles a tile. And we do it in 3 passes. Let me think about performance of 3-pass vs 1-pass: - 1-pass (1 block per row): reads input once, writes output once. Now, back to the 1-pass algorithm: ```cuda __global__ void scan_rows_kernel(float* out, const float* in, int M, int N) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int block_size = blockDim.x; if (row >= M) return; const float* input = in + row * N; float* output = out + row * N; int elems_per_thread = N / block_size; // 32768 / 512 = 64 // Phase 1: Each thread computes sum of its elements (coalesced read) float thread_sum = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < elems_per_thread; i++) { int idx = i * block_size + tid; thread_sum += input[idx]; } // Phase 2: Block-level inclusive scan of thread_sum // Step 2a: Warp-level inclusive scan float warp_sum = thread_sum; for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, warp_sum, offset); if (lane >= offset) warp_sum += y; } // Step 2b: Store warp sums in shared memory __shared__ float warp_sums[32]; // max 32 warps for 1024 threads, we use 16 for 512 threads if (lane == 31) warp_sums[warp_id] = warp_sum; __syncthreads(); // Step 2c: First warp scans warp_sums if (warp_id == 0) { float val = (lane < block_size / 32) ? warp_sums[lane] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); if (lane >= offset) val += y; } if (lane < block_size / 32) warp_sums[lane] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Step 2d: Get exclusive prefix for this thread float exclusive_prefix = 0.0f; if (warp_id > 0) exclusive_prefix = warp_sums[warp_id - 1]; // Add warp-level exclusive prefix (sum of all previous lanes in this warp) float warp_exclusive = warp_sum - thread_sum; exclusive_prefix += warp_exclusive; // Phase 3: Write prefix sums back (coalesced write) float prefix = exclusive_prefix; for (int i = 0; i < elems_per_thread; i++) { int idx = i * block_size + tid; prefix += input[idx]; output[idx] = prefix; } } ``` Wait, but in phase 3, we're reading the input again, so that's 2 reads per element. Algorithm: 1. Coalesced read: each thread reads 64 elements into shared memory 2. Each thread computes local prefix sums on its 64 shared memory elements, stores thread_sum 3. Coalesced write: each thread writes its 64 elements back Wait, but step 1 puts all elements in shared memory. Step 2 requires each thread to access its 64 elements, which for thread i are at shared memory indices i, i+512, i+1024, ..., i+63*512. These consecutive accesses by a single thread are strided by 512 * 4 = 2048 bytes. That's very bad for shared memory bank conflicts! On H100 (and most GPUs), shared memory is banked with 32 banks. A stride of 2048 bytes / 4 bytes = 512 floats = 0 mod 32? 512 / 32 = 16, so 512 floats is 16 full cycles of banks. So consecutive accesses by the same thread hit the same bank! That's 32-way bank conflict for each access! TERRIBLE performance. To avoid bank conflicts, we need adjacent threads to access adjacent banks. So in shared memory, element j should be at bank j % 32. If we read elements in a coalesced way from global memory, then thread i reads element i, i+512, i+1024, etc. If we store these at shared memory locations i * 64 + 0, i * 64 + 1, etc., then thread i accesses shared memory[i*64 + j], which is bank (i*64 + j) % 32. With i varying from 0 to 511, and 64 being divisible by 32, thread i accesses banks j % 32 when accessing the j-th element. If multiple threads access the same j simultaneously... wait, in this approach, each thread accesses its own private chunk in shared memory. There's no simultaneous access by different threads to the same element. The issue is the bank conflicts when the warp accesses shared memory. Actually, in step 2, each warp (32 threads) accesses element j of their respective chunks at the same time. Thread0 accesses smem[0*64+j], thread1 accesses smem[1*64+j], etc. The banks are (i*64+j)%32. Since 64 % 32 = 0, all threads in the warp access banks j % 32! All 32 threads access the SAME bank! That's a massive 32-way bank conflict. DISASTER. To avoid this, we need to transpose the storage: store elements such that thread i's j-th element is in bank (i + j) % 32 or something. Actually, the canonical way to avoid bank conflicts is to read in a coalesced manner and store in shared memory in a way that adjacent threads access adjacent banks when they later read. If we store element j * block_size + tid at shared memory[j * (block_size + 1) + tid], then when thread tid reads its j-th element (at shared memory[j * (block_size + 1) + tid]), the bank is [j * (block_size + 1) + tid] % 32. For threads in a warp (tid = 0..31), these are all different banks (assuming block_size+1 is odd and not multiple of 32). With block_size = 512, block_size + 1 = 513. 513 % 32 = 1. So banks are (j * 513 + tid) % 32 = (j + tid) % 32. When all threads in a warp access their j-th element simultaneously, banks are (j + tid) % 32 for tid=0..31 → all different banks! No conflicts! But the total shared memory needed is 64 * 513 * 4 bytes ≈ 131KB. That's slightly more than 128KB. But we can pad by 1 every 32 elements instead of every 512. Actually, simpler: store as smem[j * block_size + tid] with no padding, and accept the bank conflicts. Kogge-Stone uses N threads for all log2(N) steps. Each thread handles one element. For N=32768, we need 32768 threads, but a block can only have 1024 threads. So we'd need to either: - Use 32 blocks per row (1024 threads each), or - Use fewer threads but each handles multiple elements sequentially, or - Use a different algorithm. If we use 1024 threads and each handles 32 elements, a simple approach: - Load 32768 elements into shared memory (128KB) - Do a sequential scan within each thread's 32-element chunk: local_prefix[j] - Warp-level scan of the last element of each chunk - Add offsets - Write local_prefix back to shared memory This is very similar to what I described before, but now the "local processing" happens in shared memory rather than registers. For thread tid, its 32 elements in shared memory are at indices tid*32, tid*32+1, ..., tid*32+31. Banks for these: (tid*32 + j) % 32. For a warp, tid = 0..31. Banks: j % 32 for all threads in the warp. ALL threads access bank j! 32-way conflict for each j. This is terrible. To fix this, we'd need to swizzle the shared memory storage so that a thread's contiguous 32 elements hit different banks when accessed simultaneously by a warp. But a thread accesses its 32 elements one by one, not simultaneously with other threads. Wait! In step 2 (sequential scan within each thread's chunk), only ONE thread is accessing its own 32 elements at a time. There's no simultaneous access by multiple threads to nearby memory (for this step). Bank conflicts only happen when threads in the same warp access the same bank simultaneously. Since each thread does its own sequential scan independently, there are no bank conflicts during this phase! The bank conflicts would happen if we did a parallel scan where all threads access shared memory simultaneously. But in this sequential-within-thread approach, each thread accesses its own private 32-element region, and since these regions are 32 elements apart... wait, for a warp, thread 0 accesses bank 0, thread 1 accesses bank 0 (because 32 % 32 == 0), etc. OH WAIT. If threads 0..31 in a warp execute simultaneously (which they do in lockstep), and thread 0 accesses shared_mem[0], thread 1 accesses shared_mem[32], thread 2 accesses shared_mem[64], etc., then: - Bank of shared_mem[0] = 0 - Bank of shared_mem[32] = 0 - Bank of shared_mem[64] = 0 - etc. All 32 threads in the warp access bank 0 simultaneously! 32-way bank conflict! But in a sequential loop, each thread is executing independently. The threads in a warp are SIMT - they execute the same instruction. So at instruction "load shared_mem[tid*32 + j]", all threads in the warp execute this instruction together. They all access bank j. Massive conflict. So yes, the bank conflict still exists during the sequential scan phase because threads in a warp execute in lockstep. We can avoid this by swizzling the storage. If we use the padded layout discussed earlier, we can eliminate bank conflicts. Let me calculate for the padded layout: - Element i is stored at a = i + floor(i/32) - i = tid*32 + j - a = tid*32 + j + tid = tid*33 + j - a % 32 = (tid*33 + j) % 32 = (tid + j) % 32 - For a warp, tid varies from 0 to 31. a % 32 varies from j to j+31, all different. No conflict! Total shared memory: 32768 + 1024 = 33792 floats = 135KB. Under 228KB. This is very doable! So the full algorithm with swizzled shared memory: ```cuda __device__ inline int swizzle_addr(int i) { return i + (i >> 5); // i + floor(i/32) } __global__ void scan_rows_kernel(float* out, const float* in, int M, int N) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int block_size = blockDim.x; int elems_per_thread = N / block_size; // 32 for N=32768, block_size=1024 if (row >= M) return; const float* input = in + row * N; float* output = out + row * N; extern __shared__ float smem[]; int smem_size = N + (N >> 5); // total swizzled size // Phase 1: Coalesced load into swizzled shared memory for (int j = tid; j < N; j += block_size) { float val = input[j]; smem[swizzle_addr(j)] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Phase 2: Each thread does sequential prefix sum of its 32 elements float prefix = 0.0f; int start = tid * elems_per_thread; int end = start + elems_per_thread; for (int j = start; j < end; j++) { prefix += smem[swizzle_addr(j)]; smem[swizzle_addr(j)] = prefix; } // Phase 3: Block-level exclusive scan of per-thread sums float thread_sum = prefix; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; // Warp-level inclusive scan float warp_sum = thread_sum; for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, warp_sum, offset); if (lane >= offset) warp_sum += y; } __shared__ float warp_sums[32]; if (lane == 31) warp_sums[warp_id] = warp_sum; __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float val = (lane < block_size / 32) ? warp_sums[lane] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); if (lane >= offset) val += y; } if (lane < block_size / 32) warp_sums[lane] = val; } __syncthreads(); float exclusive_prefix = 0.0f; if (warp_id > 0) exclusive_prefix = warp_sums[warp_id - 1]; float warp_exclusive = warp_sum - thread_sum; exclusive_prefix += warp_exclusive; // Phase 4: Add exclusive prefix back to shared memory for (int j = start; j < end; j++) { smem[swizzle_addr(j)] += exclusive_prefix; } __syncthreads(); // Phase 5: Coalesced write from swizzled shared memory for (int j = tid; j < N; j += block_size) { output[j] = smem[swizzle_addr(j)]; } } ``` Let me check the memory access patterns: - Phase 1: threads 0..1023 load input[0..1023], then input[1024..2047], etc. Adjacent threads access adjacent elements. Perfect coalescing. - Phase 5: same but writes. Perfect coalescing. - Phase 2 and 4: each thread accesses its own 32 elements. With swizzling, no bank conflicts! Shared memory: 32768 + 1024 = 33792 floats = 135KB. Plus warp_sums[32] = negligible. Total ~136KB per block. With 228KB max shared memory per block, and the max shared memory per SM is also 228KB (of which 227KB is usable). So only 1 block can run per SM at a time. With 132 SMs on H100, that's 132 blocks concurrently. Each block handles 1 row. So 132 rows at a time. 32768 rows / 132 = 248 waves. Each wave: 132 blocks, each reads 32768*4 bytes and writes the same. Memory per wave: 132 * 32768 * 4 * 2 = 33.5MB. Assuming peak bandwidth 3.35TB/s, time per wave if fully memory bound: 33.5MB / 3.35TB/s = 10 microseconds. 248 waves * 10us = 2.48ms. Plus kernel overhead, shared memory access latency, synchronizations... maybe 3-4ms? PyTorch cumsum might be around 5-10ms. So this could be 2x faster. But wait, can we improve occupancy by using fewer threads and multiple rows per block? Consider: 512 threads per block, each thread handles 64 elements. Still 1 row per block. - We still need 136KB shared memory. - Block count: 32768. - More sequential work per thread = better for hiding latency, but same occupancy issue. What about processing 2 rows per block? Shared memory would be 272KB, too much. What about processing in smaller chunks that reuse shared memory? Like process 16KB chunks per row within a block that handles 1 row. Actually, if we process a row in 2 chunks: - Chunk 0: elements 0..16383 - Chunk 1: elements 16384..32767 - Shared memory: 16384 + 512 = 16896 floats = 66KB - Two chunks per row With 66KB shared memory, can we run 2 blocks per SM? 66KB * 2 = 132KB < 228KB. Maybe 3 blocks: 198KB < 228KB. With 3 blocks per SM and 132 SMs: 396 blocks concurrently. But blocks per row = 2, so 198 rows concurrently. 32768 rows * 2 chunks = 65536 blocks. 65536 blocks / 396 blocks per wave = 166 waves. Memory per wave: 396 blocks * 16384 floats/block * 4 bytes * 2 (read+write) = 51MB. But wait, for the 2-chunk approach, we need inter-chunk communication (the second chunk needs the sum of the first chunk). This requires either: 1. A second pass to add offsets, or 2. Some synchronization mechanism between blocks of the same row. Using approach 1: we'd need a second kernel to add offsets. This adds memory traffic and kernel launch overhead. With approach 2: we can store the partial sums in global memory and have the second chunk wait... but that's complex and slow. Actually, for the 2-chunk approach within a single block: - The block processes row elements in order: chunk 0 then chunk 1. - After chunk 0, we know the running prefix. - Process chunk 1, adding the running prefix. So 1 block does 2 chunks sequentially for 1 row. - Threads: 1024 - Shared memory: 66KB (for 16384 elements) - Each block processes 2 chunks = 1 row. But this is basically the same as the full-row shared memory approach, just using smaller shared memory and reusing it. The performance would be similar, but with the benefit of better occupancy. 32768 blocks, each with 1024 threads. 66KB shared memory per block. Max blocks per SM: min(32, 228KB/66KB=3.4) = 3 blocks. Let me also consider: can we use float4 in phases 1 and 5? Phase 1: threads 0..1023 load elements 0..1023. Thread 0 loads element 0, thread 1 loads element 1, etc. For float4: thread 0 loads elements 0..3, thread 1 loads elements 4..7? No, float4 is a 16-byte load. Actually, with coalesced scalar loads, adjacent threads load adjacent floats, which means a warp of 32 threads loads 128 bytes in a single transaction. So 32 loads by a warp coalesce into 1 transaction of 128 bytes. 1024 threads = 32 warps. Each warp does 1024 loads (32 elements/thread * 32 threads = 1024 loads per warp, but actually 32 threads per warp, each doing 32 loads sequentially). Wait, let's be precise: 1024 threads, each does 32 loads = 32768 total loads. 32 warps, each warp has 32 threads, each thread does 32 loads. A float4 load of element 0 loads bytes 0..15. Then a float4 load of element 1024 loads bytes 4096..4111. These are not contiguous, so float4 doesn't help with a single instruction. To use float4, we'd need a thread's elements to be contiguous. But for prefix sum, a thread's elements need to be contiguous. And for coalescing, adjacent threads need to access adjacent elements. These two requirements conflict for float4. Unless... we load in a coalesced pattern using float4, and then shuffle within registers or shared memory. For example: - Warp loads 128 contiguous floats (32 float4) in a coalesced manner. - Thread 0 gets elements 0..3, thread 1 gets 4..7, etc. (using some cooperative loading) - Then we transpose or redistribute so that thread 0 ends up with elements 0..31. Let me write the kernel with 1 block per row, 1024 threads, swizzled shared memory, and test it. One concern: dynamic shared memory (`extern __shared__ float smem[]`) requires knowing the size at kernel launch. For 32768 elements, we need `32768 + 1024 = 33792` floats = 135KB. Actually, I realize that I should also think about integer overflow. `32768 * 32768` is 2^30, which fits in int32. Also, `swizzle_addr` should be inlined. `i + (i >> 5)` is a simple bit shift and add. The compiler will optimize this. Let me think about whether we can avoid shared memory altogether by using a different algorithm. Actually, there's a brilliant idea from the CUB library: for a scan within a block, we can use shared memory for the warp-level aggregates and do most of the work in registers with warp shuffle. This avoids the large shared memory requirement. For our case, if we don't store the entire row in shared memory, we can process in two passes: - Pass 1: coalesced load, compute thread_sum, store thread_sum. - Block sync, warp shuffle scan of thread_sums. - Pass 2: coalesced load again, compute prefix with offset, coalesced write. No shared memory needed for the row data (except tiny bit for warp sums). This allows much higher occupancy. With 128 registers per thread and 2 blocks per SM: 256 * 2048 = 512K. H100 has 64K registers... wait, 64K per SM. 2048 threads * 64 registers = 131K. So we could have many blocks per SM. Memory traffic: 12GB. At 3.35TB/s, ~3.6ms. Can we win with 3.6ms if PyTorch does 5-10ms? Yes. And if PyTorch's implementation has extra overhead, we win by more. BUT WAIT. Can we do the single-pass with a small amount of shared memory by processing in chunks? What if we process 16 elements per thread, and use block size 512? Then each thread stores 16 floats in registers (or re-reads). If we re-read: 2 passes, 12GB traffic. If we store 16 floats: 16 registers. Plus some for temporaries = ~24 registers. Very reasonable! With 512 threads and 64 elements per thread: 64 registers. Still might be okay. Let me check register limits. H100 has 64K 32-bit registers per SM. Max 255 per thread. If we use 128 registers per thread: - 512 threads * 128 registers = 65536 = exactly 64K. - That's the hard limit per SM. So with 64 floats (64 registers) + ~20 temporaries = ~84 registers. That's well under 255 and leaves room for other uses. But the compiler might use more registers for loop unrolling, address calculations, etc. Let me try the register-array approach with 512 threads and 64 elements per thread (64-register array): ```cuda float vals[64]; float prefix = 0.0f; // Load and compute local prefix sums #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 64; j++) { int idx = j * 512 + tid; float v = input[idx]; prefix += v; vals[j] = prefix; } float thread_sum = prefix; // ... block scan to get exclusive_prefix ... // Add offset and write back prefix = exclusive_prefix; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 64; j++) { int idx = j * 512 + tid; prefix += vals[j] - (j > 0 ? vals[j-1] : 0.0f); // Wait, vals[j] already includes the sum up to j. // We need to add exclusive_prefix to vals[j]. // vals[j] = local_sum[0..j] + exclusive_prefix output[idx] = vals[j] + exclusive_prefix; } ``` Wait, `vals[j]` is the inclusive prefix sum of elements 0..j within this thread's chunk. So we just add `exclusive_prefix` to each `vals[j]`. ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 64; j++) { int idx = j * 512 + tid; output[idx] = vals[j] + exclusive_prefix; } ``` This is much simpler and only needs 1 read and 1 write! Total memory: 8GB. But the concern is that `vals[64]` takes 64 registers, and the compiler might spill to local memory if the total exceeds available registers. Let me compute more carefully. For 512 threads: - 64 array elements = 64 registers - 10 registers for warp shuffle scan - 10 registers for loop indices and addresses - Total ~84 registers per thread - 512 * 84 = 42,752 registers per block - H100 limits: 64K per SM. So 1 block uses 42K, leaving room for 1 more block (84K > 64K, so only 1 block per SM). - 2 blocks * 512 threads = 1024 threads per SM. This is 50% occupancy. - With 132 SMs: 132 blocks concurrently. - Total blocks: 32768. - Waves: 32768 / 132 = 248 waves. Occupancy is 50%, but the kernel is memory-bound. But each wave has 32 warps per SM. Hmm, actually with register arrays of 32 elements (instead of 64), the compiler might be more willing to keep them in registers. And with fewer registers per thread, more blocks could theoretically run. Wait, I made an error. For 1024 threads at 52 registers: 53,248 registers. 64K / 53K = 1.2, so still only 1 block per SM because the allocator needs some headroom and registers are allocated per block. The hardware allocates registers in chunks, and if a block needs 53K, a second block of 53K would exceed 64K. So regardless of thread count (unless very low like 128), a single block per SM is the limit when we store the row in registers. This is similar to the shared memory constraint. Both approaches give ~1 block per SM. So the choice is between: 1. Registers: 1 read, 1 write = 8GB, but compiler might spill. 2. Shared memory: 1 read, 1 write = 8GB, no spilling but bank conflicts needed. 3. Re-read: 2 reads, 1 write = 12GB, simplest code, high occupancy. Actually, we can also do a hybrid: use shared memory for a small tile and process the row in tiles. For example, tile size = 1024 elements, processed by 256 threads with 4 elements per thread. - We can process a row in 32 tiles sequentially within one block. - Shared memory for one tile: 1024 floats ≈ 4KB. - No bank conflicts if we pad properly. - Grid: 32768 blocks (1 per row), 256 threads each. - Block processes 32 tiles sequentially. Algorithm: ```cuda __global__ void scan_rows_tiled(float* out, const float* in, int M, int N) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (row >= M) return; const float* input = in + row * N; float* output = out + row * N; __shared__ float tile[1024 + 32]; // padded to avoid bank conflicts int tiles = N / 1024; float running_prefix = 0.0f; for (int t = 0; t < tiles; t++) { int tile_start = t * 1024; // Coalesced load into shared memory for (int j = tid; j < 1024; j += 256) { tile[j + (j >> 5)] = input[tile_start + j]; } __syncthreads(); // Each thread processes 4 elements sequentially int local_start = tid * 4; float prefix = running_prefix; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 4; j++) { int idx = local_start + j; prefix += tile[idx + (idx >> 5)]; } float thread_sum = prefix - running_prefix; // Block scan on thread_sum to get offsets for this tile // ... warp shuffle scan ... float exclusive_prefix = ...; // should be 0 for first thread in tile // Write back with correct prefix prefix = running_prefix + exclusive_prefix; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 4; j++) { int idx = local_start + j; prefix += tile[idx + (idx >> 5)]; output[tile_start + idx] = prefix; } __syncthreads(); running_prefix += warp_sums[last]; // total sum of this tile } } ``` Wait, there's an issue. The block scan gives us the exclusive prefix within the tile. But we also need `running_prefix` from previous tiles. So the total offset for a thread is `running_prefix + exclusive_prefix`. Also, the last element written by the block gives us the tile sum, which becomes the new `running_prefix`. For the block scan, we need the total sum of the tile. This is the inclusive scan result of the last thread. Let me trace through: 1. Load tile into shared memory (natural order, padded). 2. Each thread computes `thread_sum` = sum of its 4 elements. 3. Block-level inclusive scan of `thread_sum`. Let `inclusive_prefix` be the inclusive prefix for this thread. 4. `exclusive_prefix = inclusive_prefix - thread_sum`. 5. Total tile sum = inclusive prefix of last thread. 6. Each thread writes its 4 elements: element j's prefix = `running_prefix + exclusive_prefix + local_prefix[j]`. 7. `running_prefix += total_tile_sum`. This works! And shared memory is only ~4KB per block. With 4KB shared memory, how many blocks per SM? 228KB / 4KB = 57, but limited by 32 max blocks per SM and thread count. With 256 threads per block = 8 warps. If we can fit 4 blocks per SM: 32 warps per SM, good occupancy. 132 SMs * 4 blocks = 528 blocks concurrently. Total blocks: 32768. Waves: 32768 / 528 = 62 waves. This is much better than 248 waves! Memory traffic: - Per tile: read 1024 floats, write 1024 floats. - Per row: 32 tiles, so read 32768 floats, write 32768 floats. - Total: 8GB. Theoretical minimum: 2.39ms. This tiled sequential approach seems very promising! Let me refine the algorithm: ```cuda __global__ void scan_rows_tiled(float* out, const float* in, int M, int N) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int block_size = blockDim.x; // 256 if (row >= M) return; const float* input = in + row * N; float* output = out + row * N; // Padded shared memory for 1024 floats __shared__ float tile[1056]; // 1024 + 32 padding // For block-level scan __shared__ float warp_sums[8]; // 256/32 = 8 warps int tiles = N / 1024; float running_prefix = 0.0f; for (int t = 0; t < tiles; t++) { int tile_base = t * 1024; // Coalesced load: threads 0..255 load elements 0..255, then 256..511, etc. for (int j = tid; j < 1024; j += block_size) { tile[j + (j >> 5)] = input[tile_base + j]; } __syncthreads(); // Each thread processes 4 elements: indices tid*4, tid*4+1, tid*4+2, tid*4+3 int local_base = tid * 4; float local_vals[4]; float prefix = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 4; j++) { int smem_idx = local_base + j; local_vals[j] = tile[smem_idx + (smem_idx >> 5)]; prefix += local_vals[j]; } float thread_sum = prefix; // Warp-level inclusive scan float warp_sum = thread_sum; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, warp_sum, offset); if (lane >= offset) warp_sum += y; } if (lane == 31) warp_sums[warp_id] = warp_sum; __syncthreads(); // First warp scans warp_sums if (warp_id == 0) { float val = (lane < 8) ? warp_sums[lane] : 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); if (lane >= offset) val += y; } if (lane < 8) warp_sums[lane] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Exclusive prefix within this tile float exclusive_prefix = 0.0f; if (warp_id > 0) exclusive_prefix = warp_sums[warp_id - 1]; float warp_exclusive = warp_sum - thread_sum; exclusive_prefix += warp_exclusive; // Write back with running_prefix + exclusive_prefix prefix = running_prefix + exclusive_prefix; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 4; j++) { int smem_idx = local_base + j; prefix += local_vals[j]; output[tile_base + smem_idx] = prefix; } __syncthreads(); // Update running prefix for next tile if (tid == block_size - 1) { // running_prefix += (exclusive_prefix + thread_sum) = inclusive_prefix of last thread running_prefix = prefix; } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, in the write-back loop, `prefix` gets updated. So for the last thread, `prefix` after the loop is `running_prefix + exclusive_prefix + sum of all its 4 elements`. But the last thread's `exclusive_prefix + thread_sum` is the inclusive prefix of the last thread, which equals the total sum of the tile. However, there's an issue: `prefix` in the last thread after the loop is `running_prefix + exclusive_prefix + local_vals[0] + local_vals[1] + local_vals[2] + local_vals[3]`. And `exclusive_prefix + thread_sum` is `inclusive_prefix`. So `prefix = running_prefix + inclusive_prefix`. This is correct for the running prefix. But for other threads, `prefix` after the loop is the value of their last element, not relevant for running_prefix. There's a subtle issue with `__syncthreads()` inside the `if (tid == block_size - 1)`. We should instead just have thread 0 or the last thread compute the new running prefix and store it in shared memory. Actually, a simpler approach: ```cuda __shared__ float next_prefix; if (tid == block_size - 1) { next_prefix = running_prefix + exclusive_prefix + thread_sum; } __syncthreads(); running_prefix = next_prefix; ``` Wait, but `exclusive_prefix + thread_sum` is the inclusive prefix, and `running_prefix + inclusive_prefix` is the total prefix including this tile. Yes. Actually, we don't even need `exclusive_prefix + thread_sum`. We can just use `warp_sum + warp_exclusive... no wait.` For the last thread in the block: `inclusive_prefix = exclusive_prefix + thread_sum`. This is the total sum of the tile. So: ```cuda if (tid == block_size - 1) { next_prefix = running_prefix + exclusive_prefix + thread_sum; } __syncthreads(); running_prefix = next_prefix; ``` Wait, `exclusive_prefix + thread_sum` is the inclusive prefix. For the write to global memory: `output[tile_base + smem_idx] = prefix;` At iteration j, thread i writes to `tile_base + i*4 + j`. If we look at the writes across all threads at a fixed j: threads 0..255 write to `tile_base + 0*4 + j`, `tile_base + 1*4 + j`, ..., `tile_base + 255*4 + j`. These are spaced 4 floats = 16 bytes apart, and adjacent threads write to adjacent 16-byte blocks. For a warp (32 threads), this is 32 * 16 = 512 bytes, starting from `tile_base + j`. Since addresses are contiguous but each is 16 bytes, and H100 L1 cache line is 128 bytes, each 128-byte region contains 8 threads' writes. So a warp's writes can coalesce into 4 transactions of 128 bytes. Pretty good coalescing. Actually, can we improve write coalescing? But with 256 threads and 4 elements per thread, the write pattern is not ideal but probably acceptable. Alternatively, we can write the output in a coalesced way by using shared memory as an intermediate buffer: 1. After computing the final prefix values, write them back to shared memory. 2. Then all threads coalesced-write from shared memory to global memory. This would add one extra shared memory write phase but might improve global memory coalescing. Let me think if it's worth it. Let me add this phase: ```cuda // Phase 1: Load from global to swizzled shared memory for (int j = tid; j < 1024; j += block_size) { tile[j + (j >> 5)] = input[tile_base + j]; } __syncthreads(); // Phase 2: Each thread reads its 4 elements, computes prefix // ... Actually, the simplest is to write a general kernel and call it with the right parameters: ```cpp constexpr int BLOCK_SIZE = 256; constexpr int TILE_SIZE = 1024; constexpr int ELEMS_PER_THREAD = TILE_SIZE / BLOCK_SIZE; // 4 ``` Then in the kernel, `tiles = N / TILE_SIZE`. Actually, for better performance, we might want to process the tiles with a partially unrolled loop. Let me write the code now: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define BLOCK_SIZE 256 #define TILE_SIZE 1024 #define ELEMS_PER_THREAD (TILE_SIZE / BLOCK_SIZE) __device__ __forceinline__ int swizzle_addr(int idx) { return idx + (idx >> 5); } __global__ void scan_rows_kernel(float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, int M, int N) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; if (row >= M) return; const float* input = in + row * N; float* output = out + row * N; __shared__ float tile[TILE_SIZE + (TILE_SIZE >> 5)]; __shared__ float warp_sums[BLOCK_SIZE >> 5]; __shared__ float next_prefix; int num_tiles = N / TILE_SIZE; float running_prefix = 0.0f; for (int t = 0; t < num_tiles; ++t) { int tile_base = t * TILE_SIZE; // Phase 1: Coalesced load from global to swizzled shared memory #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < ELEMS_PER_THREAD; ++j) { int idx = j * BLOCK_SIZE + tid; tile[swizzle_addr(idx)] = input[tile_base + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Phase 2: Read local elements, compute thread_sum int local_base = tid * ELEMS_PER_THREAD; float local_vals[ELEMS_PER_THREAD]; float prefix = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < ELEMS_PER_THREAD; ++j) { int smem_idx = local_base + j; local_vals[j] = tile[swizzle_addr(smem_idx)]; prefix += local_vals[j]; } float thread_sum = prefix; // Phase 3: Block-level exclusive scan of thread_sum // Step 3a: Warp-level inclusive scan float warp_sum = thread_sum; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, warp_sum, offset); if (lane >= offset) warp_sum += y; } if (lane == 31) warp_sums[warp_id] = warp_sum; __syncthreads(); // Step 3b: First warp scans warp_sums if (warp_id == 0) { float val = (lane < (BLOCK_SIZE >> 5)) ? warp_sums[lane] : 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); if (lane >= offset) val += y; } if (lane < (BLOCK_SIZE >> 5)) warp_sums[lane] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Step 3c: Compute exclusive prefix float exclusive_prefix = 0.0f; if (warp_id > 0) exclusive_prefix = warp_sums[warp_id - 1]; float warp_exclusive = warp_sum - thread_sum; exclusive_prefix += warp_exclusive; // Phase 4: Write results back to swizzled shared memory prefix = running_prefix + exclusive_prefix; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < ELEMS_PER_THREAD; ++j) { int smem_idx = local_base + j; prefix += local_vals[j]; tile[swizzle_addr(smem_idx)] = prefix; } __syncthreads(); // Phase 5: Coalesced write from shared memory to global memory #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < ELEMS_PER_THREAD; ++j) { int idx = j * BLOCK_SIZE + tid; output[tile_base + idx] = tile[swizzle_addr(idx)]; } __syncthreads(); // Update running prefix if (tid == BLOCK_SIZE - 1) { next_prefix = prefix; } __syncthreads(); running_prefix = next_prefix; __syncthreads(); } } torch::Tensor scan_rows(torch::Tensor input, int dim) { int M = input.size(0); int N = input.size(1); auto output = torch::empty_like(input); dim3 grid(M); dim3 block(BLOCK_SIZE); size_t smem = 0; scan_rows_kernel<<<grid, block, smem>>>( output.data_ptr<float>(), input.data_ptr<float>(), M, N ); return output; } ``` Let me review this carefully. One potential issue: `warp_sums` is declared but I need to ensure its size is correct. `BLOCK_SIZE >> 5` = 256 / 32 = 8. So `warp_sums[8]`. That's fine. Another issue: in Phase 5, the write to global memory uses `tile[swizzle_addr(idx)]`. Since `idx` ranges from 0 to 1023, `swizzle_addr(idx)` ranges from 0 to 1023 + 31 = 1054. Our `tile` array size is `TILE_SIZE + (TILE_SIZE >> 5)` = 1024 + 32 = 1056. So the max index is 1054, which is within bounds. Wait, let me double check: idx = j * BLOCK_SIZE + tid Max j = 3 (since ELEMS_PER_THREAD = 4) Max tid = 255 Max idx = 3 * 256 + 255 = 1023 swizzle_addr(1023) = 1023 + (1023 >> 5) = 1023 + 31 = 1054. Array size: 1056. 1054 < 1056. Good. For local_base: local_base = tid * ELEMS_PER_THREAD = tid * 4 Max tid = 255 Max local_base = 255 * 4 = 1020 Max smem_idx = 1020 + 3 = 1023 swizzle_addr(1023) = 1054. Good. Now, one more consideration: when `if (warp_id == 0)`, only warp 0 executes this block. But the `__syncthreads()` after it ensures all warps wait. However, in CUDA, `__syncthreads()` inside a conditional can deadlock if not all threads take the same path. Since `if (warp_id == 0)` means only 1 out of 8 warps executes the code, the `__syncthreads()` inside is technically a problem because not all threads in the block reach it! Wait, no. The `__syncthreads()` after the `if (warp_id == 0)` block is outside the if, so all threads reach it. Inside the if block, there's no `__syncthreads()`. Let me check: ```cpp if (warp_id == 0) { float val = (lane < (BLOCK_SIZE >> 5)) ? warp_sums[lane] : 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); if (lane >= offset) val += y; } if (lane < (BLOCK_SIZE >> 5)) warp_sums[lane] = val; } __syncthreads(); ``` Yes, the `__syncthreads()` is outside the `if`. All threads reach it. Inside the `if`, only warp 0 does the warp shuffle, and there's no `__syncthreads()`. The `__syncwarp()` is implicit in `__shfl_up_sync` with mask 0xFFFFFFFF (all threads in the warp participate). Since only warp 0 threads satisfy `warp_id == 0`, only warp 0 executes the shuffle. But `__shfl_up_sync` requires all threads in the warp to participate. Since all 32 threads of warp 0 are in the if and execute the shuffle, it's fine. Other warps don't execute the shuffle at all. This is correct. Wait, but there's a potential issue: `warp_sums[lane]` where `lane` ranges from 0 to 31. And `warp_sums` has size `BLOCK_SIZE >> 5` = 8. In the code: ```cpp float val = (lane < (BLOCK_SIZE >> 5)) ? warp_sums[lane] : 0.0f; ``` This checks `lane < 8` before accessing `warp_sums[lane]`. Good.
